<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snail-wiki-120-Zachi-Evenor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1663" data-file-width="1866" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Snail-wiki-120-Zachi-Evenor.jpg/220px-Snail-wiki-120-Zachi-Evenor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Snail-wiki-120-Zachi-Evenor.jpg/330px-Snail-wiki-120-Zachi-Evenor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Snail-wiki-120-Zachi-Evenor.jpg/440px-Snail-wiki-120-Zachi-Evenor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snail-wiki-120-Zachi-Evenor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Cornu_aspersum" title="Cornu aspersum">Cornu aspersum</a></i> (formerly <i>Helix aspersa</i>) – a common land <a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snail</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>molluscs</b> (or <b>mollusks</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[note 1]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɒ/ short 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>) compose the large <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phylum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> animals known as the <b>Mollusca</b>. Around 85,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of molluscs are recognized. Molluscs are the largest <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">marine</a> phylum, comprising about 23% of all the named marine <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>. Numerous molluscs also live in <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_mollusc" title="Freshwater mollusc">freshwater</a> and terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a>. They are highly diverse, not just in size and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomical" title="Anatomical">anatomical</a> structure, but also in behaviour and in habitat. The phylum is typically divided into 9 or 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxonomic" title="Taxonomic">taxonomic</a> <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">classes</a>, of which two are entirely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct" title="Extinct">extinct</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">Cephalopod</a> molluscs, such as <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a>, are among the most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurobiology" title="Neurobiology">neurologically advanced</a> of all <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>—and either the <a href="/wiki/Giant_squid" title="Giant squid">giant squid</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Colossal_squid" title="Colossal squid">colossal squid</a> is the largest known invertebrate species. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a> (<a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snails</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slug" title="Slug">slugs</a>) are by far the most numerous molluscs in terms of classified species, and account for 80% of the total. The scientific study of molluscs is called <a href="/wiki/Malacology" title="Malacology">malacology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The three most universal features defining modern molluscs are a <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(mollusc)" title="Mantle (mollusc)">mantle</a> with a significant cavity used for breathing and <a href="/wiki/Excretion" title="Excretion">excretion</a>, the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Radula" title="Radula">radula</a> (except for bivalves), and the structure of the <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a>. Other than these things, molluscs express great morphological diversity, so many textbooks base their descriptions on a "hypothetical ancestral mollusc" (see image below). This has a single, "<a href="/wiki/Limpet" title="Limpet">limpet</a>-like" <a href="/wiki/Gastropod_shell" title="Gastropod shell">shell</a> on top, which is made of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a> reinforced with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>, and is secreted by a mantle covering the whole upper surface. The underside of the animal consists of a single muscular "foot". Although molluscs are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coelomate" title="Coelomate">coelomates</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Coelom" title="Coelom">coelom</a> tends to be small. The main body cavity is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemocoel" title="Hemocoel">hemocoel</a> through which <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> circulates; their <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory systems</a> are mainly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_circulatory_system" title="Open circulatory system">open</a>. The "generalized" mollusc's feeding system consists of a rasping "tongue", the radula, and a complex digestive system in which exuded <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> and microscopic, muscle-powered "hairs" called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilia" title="Cilia">cilia</a> play various important roles. The generalized mollusc has two paired <a href="/wiki/Ventral_nerve_cord" title="Ventral nerve cord">nerve cords</a>, or three in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>, in species that have one, encircles the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a>. Most molluscs have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eyes" title="Eyes">eyes</a>, and all have sensors to detect chemicals, vibrations, and touch. The simplest type of molluscan <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_system" title="Reproductive system">reproductive system</a> relies on <a href="/wiki/External_fertilization" title="External fertilization">external fertilization</a>, but more complex variations occur. All produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">eggs</a>, from which may emerge <a href="/wiki/Trochophore" title="Trochophore">trochophore</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Larvae" title="Larvae">larvae</a>, more complex <a href="/wiki/Veliger" title="Veliger">veliger</a> larvae, or miniature adults.</p>
<p>Good evidence exists for the appearance of gastropods, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalopods" title="Cephalopods">cephalopods</a> and bivalves in the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> period <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=541–485.4">541 to 485.4</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>. However, the evolutionary history both of molluscs' emergence from the ancestral <a href="/wiki/Lophotrochozoa" title="Lophotrochozoa">Lophotrochozoa</a> and of their diversification into the well-known living and <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> forms are still subjects of vigorous debate among scientists.</p>
<p>Molluscs have been and still are an important food source for <a href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_human" title="Anatomically modern human">anatomically modern humans</a>, but with a risk of food poisoning from toxins that accumulate in molluscs under certain conditions, and many countries have regulations to reduce this risk. Molluscs have, for centuries, also been the source of important luxury goods, notably <a href="/wiki/Pearl" title="Pearl">pearls</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mother_of_pearl" title="Mother of pearl">mother of pearl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyrian_purple" title="Tyrian purple">Tyrian purple</a> dye, and <a href="/wiki/Sea_silk" title="Sea silk">sea silk</a>. Their shells have also been used as <a href="/wiki/Money" title="Money">money</a> in some preindustrial societies. Mollusc species can also represent hazards or pests for human activities. The bite of the <a href="/wiki/Blue-ringed_octopus" title="Blue-ringed octopus">blue-ringed octopus</a> is often fatal, and that of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octopus_apollyon" title="Octopus apollyon">Octopus apollyon</a></i> causes <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> that can last for over a month. Stings from a few species of large tropical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cone_shell" title="Cone shell">cone shells</a> can also kill, but their sophisticated, though easily produced, venoms have become important tools in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurological" title="Neurological">neurological</a> research. <a href="/wiki/Schistosomiasis" title="Schistosomiasis">Schistosomiasis</a> (also known as bilharzia, bilharziosis or snail fever) is transmitted to humans via water snail hosts, and affects about 200 million people. Snails and slugs can also be serious agricultural pests, and accidental or deliberate introduction of some snail species into new environments has seriously damaged some <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The words <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mollusc" title="wikt:mollusc">mollusc</a> and mollusk are both derived from the French <i>mollusque</i>, which originated from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>molluscus</i>, from <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mollis" title="wikt:mollis">mollis</a></i>, soft. <i>Molluscus</i> was itself an adaptation of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s τα μαλακά <i>ta malaká</i>, "the soft things", which he applied to <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name <b>Molluscoida</b> was formerly used to denote a division of the animal kingdom containing the <a href="/wiki/Brachiopod" title="Brachiopod">brachiopods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bryozoa" title="Bryozoa">bryozoans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tunicate" title="Tunicate">tunicates</a>, the members of the three groups having been supposed to somewhat resemble the molluscs. As it is now known these groups have no relation to molluscs, and very little to one another, the name Molluscoida has been abandoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The most universal features of the body structure of molluscs are a <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(mollusc)" title="Mantle (mollusc)">mantle</a> with a significant cavity used for <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">breathing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Excretion" title="Excretion">excretion</a>, and the organization of the nervous system. The most abundant metallic element in molluscs is <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Molluscs have developed such a varied range of body structures, it is difficult to find <a href="/wiki/Synapomorphy" title="Synapomorphy">synapomorphies</a> (defining characteristics) to apply to all modern groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GiribetOkusuEtAlMolluscsWithSeriallyRepeatedStructures_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-GiribetOkusuEtAlMolluscsWithSeriallyRepeatedStructures-7">[6]</a></sup> The most general characteristic of molluscs is they are unsegmented and bilaterally symmetrical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook-8">[7]</a></sup> The following are present in all modern molluscs:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BruscaBrusca2003P702_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-BruscaBrusca2003P702-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorsoventral" title="Dorsoventral">dorsal</a> part of the body wall is a <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(mollusc)" title="Mantle (mollusc)">mantle</a> (or pallium) which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secrete" title="Secrete">secretes</a> <a href="/wiki/Calcareous" title="Calcareous">calcareous</a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/spicule" title="wikt:spicule">spicules</a>, plates or shells. It overlaps the body with enough spare room to form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mantle_cavity" title="Mantle cavity">mantle cavity</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genital" title="Genital">genitals</a> open into the mantle cavity.</li>
<li>There are two pairs of main <a href="/wiki/Ventral_nerve_cord" title="Ventral nerve cord">nerve cords</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Other characteristics that commonly appear in textbooks have significant exceptions:</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>

<th><a href="/wiki/Aplacophora" title="Aplacophora">Aplacophora</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaAplacophora_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaAplacophora-11">[10]</a></sup></th>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity">Diversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Diversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cypraea_chinensis_with_partially_extended_mantle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1188" data-file-width="1788" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Cypraea_chinensis_with_partially_extended_mantle.jpg/220px-Cypraea_chinensis_with_partially_extended_mantle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Cypraea_chinensis_with_partially_extended_mantle.jpg/330px-Cypraea_chinensis_with_partially_extended_mantle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Cypraea_chinensis_with_partially_extended_mantle.jpg/440px-Cypraea_chinensis_with_partially_extended_mantle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cypraea_chinensis_with_partially_extended_mantle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
About 80% of all known mollusc species are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a> (<a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snails</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slug" title="Slug">slugs</a>), including this <a href="/wiki/Cowry" title="Cowry">cowry</a> (a sea snail).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PonderWinstonLindberg_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-PonderWinstonLindberg-18">[17]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Estimates of accepted described living species of molluscs vary from 50,000 to a maximum of 120,000 species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman_2009_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman_2009-1">[1]</a></sup> In 1969 David Nicol estimated the probable total number of living molluscs at 107,000 of which were about 12,000 fresh-water gastropods and 35,000 terrestrial. The Bivalvia would comprise about 14% of the total and the other five classes less than 2% of the living molluscs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> In 2009, Chapman estimated the number of described living species at 85,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman_2009_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman_2009-1">[1]</a></sup> Haszprunar in 2001 estimated about 93,000 named species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haszprunar2001MolluscsInEncOfLifeSci_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haszprunar2001MolluscsInEncOfLifeSci-20">[19]</a></sup> which include 23% of all named marine organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HancockRecognisingResearch_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-HancockRecognisingResearch-21">[20]</a></sup> Molluscs are second only to <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> in numbers of living animal species<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PonderWinstonLindberg_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-PonderWinstonLindberg-18">[17]</a></sup>—far behind the arthropods' 1,113,000 but well ahead of <a href="/wiki/Chordate" title="Chordate">chordates</a>' 52,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004FrontEnd1_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004FrontEnd1-22">[21]</a></sup> About 200,000 living species in total are estimated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman_2009_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman_2009-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PonderLindberg2004Phylogeny_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-PonderLindberg2004Phylogeny-23">[22]</a></sup> and 70,000 fossil species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BruscaBrusca2003P702_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-BruscaBrusca2003P702-9">[8]</a></sup> although the total number of mollusc species ever to have existed, whether or not preserved, must be many times greater than the number alive today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Molluscs have more varied forms than any other animal <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phylum</a>. They include <a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snails</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slug" title="Slug">slugs</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a>; <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">clams</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a>; <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squids</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a>; and other lesser-known but similarly distinctive subgroups. The majority of species still live in the oceans, from the seashores to the <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_zone" title="Abyssal zone">abyssal zone</a>, but some form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater_molluscs" title="Freshwater molluscs">significant part of the freshwater fauna</a> and the terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>. Molluscs are extremely diverse in <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> regions, but can be found at all <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GiribetOkusuEtAlMolluscsWithSeriallyRepeatedStructures_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-GiribetOkusuEtAlMolluscsWithSeriallyRepeatedStructures-7">[6]</a></sup> About 80% of all known mollusc species are gastropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PonderWinstonLindberg_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-PonderWinstonLindberg-18">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">Cephalopoda</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopuses</a> are among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurobiology" title="Neurobiology">neurologically</a> most advanced of all invertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BarnesCalowEtAl2001InvertebratesSynthesis_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-BarnesCalowEtAl2001InvertebratesSynthesis-25">[24]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Giant_squid" title="Giant squid">giant squid</a>, which until recently had not been observed alive in its adult form,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> is one of the largest <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>, but a recently caught specimen of the <a href="/wiki/Colossal_squid" title="Colossal squid">colossal squid</a>, 10 m (33 ft) long and weighing 500 kg (1,100 lb), may have overtaken it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Freshwater and terrestrial molluscs appear exceptionally vulnerable to extinction. Estimates of the numbers of nonmarine molluscs vary widely, partly because many regions have not been thoroughly surveyed. There is also a shortage of specialists who can identify all the animals in any one area to species. However, in 2004 the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a> of Threatened Species included nearly 2,000 endangered nonmarine molluscs. For comparison, the great majority of mollusc species are marine, but only 41 of these appeared on the 2004 Red List. About 42% of recorded extinctions since the year 1500 are of molluscs, consisting almost entirely of nonmarine species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="A_.22generalized_mollusc.22">A "generalized mollusc"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title='Edit section: A "generalized mollusc"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mollusc_shell" title="Mollusc shell">Mollusc shell</a></div>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archimollusc-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2492" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Archimollusc-en.svg/500px-Archimollusc-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Archimollusc-en.svg/750px-Archimollusc-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Archimollusc-en.svg/1000px-Archimollusc-en.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archimollusc-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Anatomical diagram of a hypothetical ancestral mollusc</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because of the great range of anatomical diversity among molluscs, many textbooks start the subject of molluscan anatomy by describing what is called an <i>archi-mollusc</i>, <i>hypothetical generalized mollusc</i>, or <i>hypothetical ancestral mollusc</i> (<i>HAM</i>) to illustrate the most common features found within the phylum. These species reproduce through binary fission, much like a sea star. The depiction is rather similar to modern <a href="/wiki/Monoplacophora" title="Monoplacophora">monoplacophorans</a>, and some suggest it may resemble very early molluscs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GiribetOkusuEtAlMolluscsWithSeriallyRepeatedStructures_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-GiribetOkusuEtAlMolluscsWithSeriallyRepeatedStructures-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaMonoplacophora_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaMonoplacophora-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Healy2001MolluscaInvertebrateZoology_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Healy2001MolluscaInvertebrateZoology-29">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The generalized mollusc is bilaterally symmetrical and has a single, "<a href="/wiki/Limpet" title="Limpet">limpet</a>-like" <a href="/wiki/Gastropod_shell" title="Gastropod shell">shell</a> on top. The shell is secreted by a mantle covering the upper surface. The underside consists of a single muscular "foot".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup> The visceral mass, or visceropallium, is the soft, nonmuscular metabolic region of the mollusc. It contains the body organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mantle_and_mantle_cavity">Mantle and mantle cavity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mantle and mantle cavity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The mantle cavity, a fold in the mantle, encloses a significant amount of space. It is lined with epidermis, and is exposed, according to <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a>, to sea, fresh water or air. The cavity was at the rear in the earliest molluscs, but its position now varies from group to group. The <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a>, a pair of <a href="/wiki/Osphradium" title="Osphradium">osphradia</a> (chemical sensors) in the incoming "lane", the hindmost pair of <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a> and the exit openings of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nephridia" title="Nephridia">nephridia</a> ("kidneys") and <a href="/wiki/Gonad" title="Gonad">gonads</a> (reproductive organs) are in the mantle cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup> The whole soft body of bivalves lies within an enlarged mantle cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shell">Shell<span id="Shell"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Shell">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mollusc_shell" title="Mollusc shell">Mollusc shell</a></div>
<p>The mantle edge secretes a shell (secondarily absent in a number of taxonomic groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Nudibranch" title="Nudibranch">nudibranchs</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook-8">[7]</a></sup>) that consists of mainly <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conchiolin" title="Conchiolin">conchiolin</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> hardened with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porter2007_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Porter2007-30">[29]</a></sup> except the outermost layer in almost all cases is all conchiolin (see <a href="/wiki/Periostracum" title="Periostracum">periostracum</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup> Molluscs never use phosphate to construct their hard parts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> with the questionable exception of <i><a href="/wiki/Cobcrephora" title="Cobcrephora">Cobcrephora</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cherns2004_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cherns2004-32">[31]</a></sup> While most mollusc shells are composed mainly of <a href="/wiki/Aragonite" title="Aragonite">aragonite</a>, those gastropods that lay eggs with a hard shell use <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a> (sometimes with traces of aragonite) to construct the eggshells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The shell consists of three layers: the outer layer (the <a href="/wiki/Periostracum" title="Periostracum">periostracum</a>) made of organic matter, a middle layer made of columnar <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a>, and an inner layer consisting of laminated calcite, often <a href="/wiki/Nacre" title="Nacre">nacreous</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foot">Foot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Foot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:384px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/SeaSnails.ogv/384px--SeaSnails.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:384px;height:288px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="50.04" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="SeaSnails.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/SeaSnails.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 384 × 288 (335 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="384" data-height="288" data-bandwidth="334908" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:SeaSnails.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/SeaSnails.ogv/220px--SeaSnails.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/SeaSnails.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SeaSnails.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 50-second video of snails (most likely <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natica_chemnitzi" title="Natica chemnitzi">Natica chemnitzi</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Cerithium" title="Cerithium">Cerithium</a> stercusmuscaram</i>) feeding on the sea floor in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_California" title="Gulf of California">Gulf of California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Pe%C3%B1asco" title="Puerto Peñasco">Puerto Peñasco</a>, Mexico</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The underside consists of a muscular foot, which has adapted to different purposes in different classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca11_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca11-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:4</sup> The foot carries a pair of <a href="/wiki/Statocyst" title="Statocyst">statocysts</a>, which act as balance sensors. In gastropods, it secretes <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> as a lubricant to aid movement. In forms having only a top shell, such as <a href="/wiki/Limpet" title="Limpet">limpets</a>, the foot acts as a sucker attaching the animal to a hard surface, and the vertical muscles clamp the shell down over it; in other molluscs, the vertical muscles pull the foot and other exposed soft parts into the shell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup> In bivalves, the foot is adapted for burrowing into the sediment;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca11_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca11-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:4</sup> in cephalopods it is used for jet propulsion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca11_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca11-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:4</sup> and the tentacles and arms are derived from the foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circulatory_system">Circulatory system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Circulatory system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Molluscs' <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory systems</a> are mainly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_circulatory_system" title="Open circulatory system">open</a>. Although molluscs are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coelomate" title="Coelomate">coelomates</a>, their <a href="/wiki/Coelom" title="Coelom">coeloms</a> are reduced to fairly small spaces enclosing the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> and gonads. The main body cavity is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemocoel" title="Hemocoel">hemocoel</a> through which <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coelom#Coelomic_fluid" title="Coelom">coelomic fluid</a> circulate and which encloses most of the other internal organs. These hemocoelic spaces act as an efficient <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_skeleton" title="Hydrostatic skeleton">hydrostatic skeleton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook-8">[7]</a></sup> The blood contains the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_pigment" title="Respiratory pigment">respiratory pigment</a> <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">hemocyanin</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>-carrier. The heart consists of one or more pairs of atria (<a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">auricles</a>), which receive oxygenated blood from the gills and pump it to the <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">ventricle</a>, which pumps it into the <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a> (main <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">artery</a>), which is fairly short and opens into the hemocoel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-8"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The atria of the heart also function as part of the <a href="/wiki/Excretory_system" title="Excretory system">excretory system</a> by filtering waste products out of the blood and dumping it into the coelom as <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a>. A pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nephridia" title="Nephridia">nephridia</a> ("little kidneys") to the rear of and connected to the coelom extracts any re-usable materials from the urine and dumps additional waste products into it, and then ejects it via tubes that discharge into the mantle cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-9"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiration">Respiration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Respiration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most molluscs have only one pair of gills, or even only one gill. Generally, the gills are rather like feathers in shape, although some species have gills with filaments on only one side. They divide the mantle cavity so water enters near the bottom and exits near the top. Their filaments have three kinds of cilia, one of which drives the water current through the mantle cavity, while the other two help to keep the gills clean. If the osphradia detect noxious chemicals or possibly <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> entering the mantle cavity, the gills' cilia may stop beating until the unwelcome intrusions have ceased. Each gill has an incoming blood vessel connected to the hemocoel and an outgoing one to the heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-10"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eating.2C_digestion.2C_and_excretion">Eating, digestion, and excretion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Eating, digestion, and excretion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snail_radula_working.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="197" data-file-width="355" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Snail_radula_working.png/220px-Snail_radula_working.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Snail_radula_working.png/330px-Snail_radula_working.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/19/Snail_radula_working.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snail_radula_working.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Snail <a href="/wiki/Radula" title="Radula">radula</a> at work<br/>
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#00a0c6; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> = Food</span>     <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#8d9899; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> = <a href="/wiki/Radula" title="Radula">Radula</a></span><br/>
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#ce8059; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> = Muscles</span><br/>
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#d2d268; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> = <a href="/wiki/Odontophore" title="Odontophore">Odontophore</a> "belt"</span></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Members of the mollusc family use <a href="/wiki/Intracellular_digestion" title="Intracellular digestion">intracellular digestion</a> to function. Most molluscs have muscular mouths with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radulae" title="Radulae">radulae</a>, "tongues", bearing many rows of chitinous teeth, which are replaced from the rear as they wear out. The radula primarily functions to scrape <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> off rocks, and is associated with the <a href="/wiki/Odontophore" title="Odontophore">odontophore</a>, a cartilaginous supporting organ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook-8">[7]</a></sup> The radula is unique to the molluscs and has no equivalent in any other animal.</p>
<p>Molluscs' mouths also contain <a href="/wiki/Gland" title="Gland">glands</a> that secrete slimy <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a>, to which the food sticks. Beating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilia" title="Cilia">cilia</a> (tiny "hairs") drive the mucus towards the stomach, so the mucus forms a long string called a "food string".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-11"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the tapered rear end of the stomach and projecting slightly into the hindgut is the prostyle, a backward-pointing cone of <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a> and mucus, which is rotated by further cilia so it acts as a bobbin, winding the mucus string onto itself. Before the mucus string reaches the prostyle, the acidity of the stomach makes the mucus less sticky and frees particles from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-12"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The particles are sorted by yet another group of cilia, which send the smaller particles, mainly minerals, to the prostyle so eventually they are excreted, while the larger ones, mainly food, are sent to the stomach's <a href="/wiki/Cecum" title="Cecum">cecum</a> (a pouch with no other exit) to be digested. The sorting process is by no means perfect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-13"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Periodically, circular muscles at the hindgut's entrance pinch off and excrete a piece of the prostyle, preventing the prostyle from growing too large. The anus, in the part of the mantle cavity, is swept by the outgoing "lane" of the current created by the gills. Carnivorous molluscs usually have simpler digestive systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-14"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the head has largely disappeared in bivalves, the mouth has been equipped with labial palps (two on each side of the mouth) to collect the <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">detritus</a> from its mucus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nervous_system">Nervous system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Nervous system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gastropod_nervous_system.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="440" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Gastropod_nervous_system.gif/220px-Gastropod_nervous_system.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Gastropod_nervous_system.gif/330px-Gastropod_nervous_system.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d2/Gastropod_nervous_system.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gastropod_nervous_system.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Simplified diagram of the mollusc nervous system</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The cephalic molluscs have two pairs of main <a href="/wiki/Ventral_nerve_cord" title="Ventral nerve cord">nerve cords</a> organized around a number of paired ganglia, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viscera" title="Viscera">visceral</a> cords serving the internal organs and the pedal ones serving the foot. Most pairs of corresponding ganglia on both sides of the body are linked by <a href="/wiki/Commissure" title="Commissure">commissures</a> (relatively large bundles of nerves). The ganglia above the gut are the cerebral, the pleural, and the visceral, which are located above the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a> (gullet). The pedal ganglia, which control the foot, are below the esophagus and their commissure and connectives to the cerebral and pleural ganglia surround the esophagus in a <i>circumesophageal nerve ring</i> or <i>nerve collar</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-15"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The acephalic molluscs (bivalves) also have this ring but it is less obvious and less important. The bivalves have only three pairs of ganglia— cerebral, pedal, and visceral— with the visceral as the largest and most important of the three functioning as the principal center of "thinking". Some such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scallops" title="Scallops">scallops</a> have eyes around the edges of their shells which connect to a pair of looped nerves and which provide the ability to distinguish between light and shadow.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div id="annotation_145x62" style="position:absolute; left:145px; top:62px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;">Stomach</span></div>
<div id="annotation_40x85" style="position:absolute; left:40px; top:85px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;">Mouth</span></div>
<div id="annotation_5x105" style="position:absolute; left:5px; top:105px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;">Metatroch (cilia)</span></div>
<div id="annotation_40x125" style="position:absolute; left:40px; top:125px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;">Mesoderm</span></div>
<div id="annotation_220x147" style="position:absolute; left:220px; top:147px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;">Anus</span></div>
<div id="annotation_5x155" style="position:absolute; left:5px; top:155px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><span style="font-weight:900; color:#333333">///</span> = cilia</span></div>
</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trochophore_larva_01.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="268" data-file-width="309" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Trochophore_larva_01.png/200px-Trochophore_larva_01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Trochophore_larva_01.png/300px-Trochophore_larva_01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b1/Trochophore_larva_01.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Trochophore_larva_01.png" title="File:Trochophore larva 01.png"></a></div>
Trochophore larva<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The simplest molluscan reproductive system relies on <a href="/wiki/External_fertilization" title="External fertilization">external fertilization</a>, but with more complex variations. All produce eggs, from which may emerge <a href="/wiki/Trochophore" title="Trochophore">trochophore</a> larvae, more complex <a href="/wiki/Veliger" title="Veliger">veliger</a> larvae, or miniature adults. Two <a href="/wiki/Gonad" title="Gonad">gonads</a> sit next to the <a href="/wiki/Coelom" title="Coelom">coelom</a>, a small cavity that surrounds the heart, into which they shed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovum" title="Ovum">ova</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a>. The nephridia extract the gametes from the coelom and emit them into the mantle cavity. Molluscs that use such a system remain of one sex all their lives and rely on <a href="/wiki/External_fertilization" title="External fertilization">external fertilization</a>. Some molluscs use <a href="/wiki/Internal_fertilization" title="Internal fertilization">internal fertilization</a> and/or are <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">hermaphrodites</a>, functioning as both sexes; both of these methods require more complex reproductive systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen_10-16"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGen-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most basic molluscan <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larva</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Trochophore" title="Trochophore">trochophore</a>, which is <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">planktonic</a> and feeds on floating food particles by using the two bands of cilia around its "equator" to sweep food into the mouth, which uses more cilia to drive them into the stomach, which uses further cilia to expel undigested remains through the anus. New tissue grows in the bands of <a href="/wiki/Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm">mesoderm</a> in the interior, so the apical tuft and anus are pushed further apart as the animal grows. The trochophore stage is often succeeded by a <a href="/wiki/Veliger" title="Veliger">veliger</a> stage in which the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Prototroch&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Prototroch (page does not exist)">prototroch</a>, the "equatorial" band of cilia nearest the apical tuft, develops into the velum ("veil"), a pair of cilia-bearing lobes with which the larva swims. Eventually, the larva sinks to the seafloor and <a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis">metamorphoses</a> into the adult form. While metamorphosis is the usual state in molluscs, the cephalopods differ in exhibiting direct development: the hatchling is a 'miniaturized' form of the adult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marin2004_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marin2004-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most molluscs are herbivorous, grazing on algae or filter feeders. For those grazing, two feeding strategies are predominant. Some feed on microscopic, filamentous algae, often using their radula as a 'rake' to comb up filaments from the sea floor. Others feed on macroscopic 'plants' such as kelp, rasping the plant surface with its radula. To employ this strategy, the plant has to be large enough for the mollusc to 'sit' on, so smaller macroscopic plants are not as often eaten as their larger counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steneck1982_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steneck1982-38">[37]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filter_feeders" title="Filter feeders">Filter feeders</a> are molluscs that feed by straining suspended matter and food particle from water, typically by passing the water over their gills. Most bivalves are filter feeders.</p>
<p>Cephalopods are primarily predatory, and the radula takes a secondary role to the jaws and tentacles in food acquisition. The monoplacophoran <i>Neopilina</i> uses its radula in the usual fashion, but its diet includes protists such as the <a href="/wiki/Xenophyophore" title="Xenophyophore">xenophyophore</a> <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Stannophyllum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Stannophyllum (page does not exist)">Stannophyllum</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Saccoglossa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Saccoglossa (page does not exist)">Sacoglossan</a> sea-slugs suck the sap from algae, using their one-row radula to pierce the cell walls,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen1993_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen1993-40">[39]</a></sup> whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorid" title="Dorid">dorid</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nudibranchs" title="Nudibranchs">nudibranchs</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Vetigastropoda" title="Vetigastropoda">Vetigastropoda</a> feed on sponges<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> and others feed on hydroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> (An extensive list of molluscs with unusual feeding habits is available in the appendix of <cite class="citation journal">GRAHAM, A. (1955). <a class="external text" href="http://mollus.oxfordjournals.org/content/31/3-4/144.short" rel="nofollow">"Molluscan diets"</a>. <i>Journal of Molluscan Studies</i>. <b>31</b> (3–4): 144.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMollusca&amp;rft.atitle=Molluscan+diets&amp;rft.au=GRAHAM%2C+A.&amp;rft.date=1955&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fmollus.oxfordjournals.org%2Fcontent%2F31%2F3-4%2F144.short&amp;rft.issue=3%934&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Molluscan+Studies&amp;rft.pages=144&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=31"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>.)</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_mollusc_orders" title="List of mollusc orders">List of mollusc orders</a></div>
<p>Opinions vary about the number of <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">classes</a> of molluscs; for example, the table below shows eight living classes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haszprunar2001MolluscsInEncOfLifeSci_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Haszprunar2001MolluscsInEncOfLifeSci-20">[19]</a></sup> and two extinct ones. Although they are unlikely to form a clade, some older works combine the <a href="/wiki/Caudofoveata" title="Caudofoveata">Caudofoveata</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solenogasters" title="Solenogasters">solenogasters</a> into one class, the <a href="/wiki/Aplacophora" title="Aplacophora">Aplacophora</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaAplacophora_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaAplacophora-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Healy2001MolluscaInvertebrateZoology_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Healy2001MolluscaInvertebrateZoology-29">[28]</a></sup> Two of the commonly recognized "classes" are known only from fossils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PonderWinstonLindberg_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-PonderWinstonLindberg-18">[17]</a></sup></p>

</center>
<p>Classification into higher taxa for these groups has been and remains problematic. A phylogenetic study suggests the Polyplacophora form a clade with a monophyletic Aplacophora.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kocot2011_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kocot2011-49">[48]</a></sup> Additionally, it suggests a sister taxon relationship exists between the Bivalvia and the Gastropoda.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_molluscs" title="Evolution of molluscs">Evolution of molluscs</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_cephalopods" title="Evolution of cephalopods">Evolution of cephalopods</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monachoides_vicinus_dart_lateral.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="828" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Monachoides_vicinus_dart_lateral.jpg/240px-Monachoides_vicinus_dart_lateral.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Monachoides_vicinus_dart_lateral.jpg/360px-Monachoides_vicinus_dart_lateral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Monachoides_vicinus_dart_lateral.jpg/480px-Monachoides_vicinus_dart_lateral.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_record">Fossil record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fossil record">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Good evidence exists for the appearance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> period <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=541–485.4">541 to 485.4</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, or even earlier. The first molluscs appear to have been originated earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> However, the evolutionary history both of the emergence of molluscs from the ancestral group <a href="/wiki/Lophotrochozoa" title="Lophotrochozoa">Lophotrochozoa</a>, and of their diversification into the well-known living and <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> forms, is still vigorously debated.</p>
<p>Debate occurs about whether some <a href="/wiki/Ediacaran" title="Ediacaran">Ediacaran</a> and Early <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> fossils really are molluscs. <i><a href="/wiki/Kimberella" title="Kimberella">Kimberella</a></i>, from about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=555">555</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, has been described by some paleontologists as "mollusc-like",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fedonkin1997_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fedonkin1997-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FedonkinEtAl2007NewDataOnKimberella_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FedonkinEtAl2007NewDataOnKimberella-53">[52]</a></sup> but others are unwilling to go further than "probable <a href="/wiki/Bilateria" title="Bilateria">bilaterian</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butterfield2006_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Butterfield2006-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sigwart2007_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sigwart2007-55">[54]</a></sup> There is an even sharper debate about whether <i><a href="/wiki/Wiwaxia" title="Wiwaxia">Wiwaxia</a></i>, from about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=505">505</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, was a mollusc, and much of this centers on whether its feeding apparatus was a type of <a href="/wiki/Radula" title="Radula">radula</a> or more similar to that of some <a href="/wiki/Polychaete" title="Polychaete">polychaete</a> worms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butterfield2006_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Butterfield2006-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CaronScheltemaSchanderRudkin2006_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-CaronScheltemaSchanderRudkin2006-56">[55]</a></sup> Nicholas Butterfield, who opposes the idea that <i>Wiwaxia</i> was a mollusc, has written that earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microfossil" title="Microfossil">microfossils</a> from <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=515–510">515 to 510</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span> are fragments of a genuinely mollusc-like radula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butterfield2008EarlyCambrianRadula_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Butterfield2008EarlyCambrianRadula-57">[56]</a></sup> This appears to contradict the concept that the ancestral molluscan radula was mineralized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cruz1998_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cruz1998-58">[57]</a></sup></p>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptunea_despecta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="550" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Neptunea_despecta.jpg/120px-Neptunea_despecta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Neptunea_despecta.jpg/180px-Neptunea_despecta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Neptunea_despecta.jpg/240px-Neptunea_despecta.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptunea_despecta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spirally coiled shells appear in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGastropoda_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGastropoda-14">[13]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>However, the <a href="/wiki/Helcionellid" title="Helcionellid">Helcionellids</a>, which first appear over <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=540">540</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span> in Early Cambrian rocks from <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> and China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parkhaev_2007_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parkhaev_2007-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steiner2007_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steiner2007-60">[59]</a></sup> are thought to be early molluscs with rather snail-like shells. Shelled molluscs therefore predate the earliest <a href="/wiki/Trilobite" title="Trilobite">trilobites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RunnegarPojeta1974_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-RunnegarPojeta1974-48">[47]</a></sup> Although most helcionellid fossils are only a few millimeters long, specimens a few centimeters long have also been found, most with more <a href="/wiki/Limpet" title="Limpet">limpet</a>-like shapes. The tiny specimens have been suggested to be juveniles and the larger ones adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mus2008_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mus2008-61">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some analyses of helcionellids concluded these were the earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> However, other scientists are not convinced these Early Cambrian fossils show clear signs of the <a href="/wiki/Torsion_(gastropod)" title="Torsion (gastropod)">torsion</a> that identifies modern gastropods twists the internal organs so the anus lies above the head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGastropoda_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGastropoda-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fr.C3.BDdaN.C3.BCtzelWagner2008_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fr.C3.BDdaN.C3.BCtzelWagner2008-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright" style=";">
<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:217px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:152px; overflow:hidden; position:relative; background-color:white;">
<div style=";left:0px; top:0px; width:150px; position:absolute;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png" title="Septa and siphuncle in nautiloid shell"><img alt="" data-file-height="598" data-file-width="607" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png/150px-Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png/225px-Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png/300px-Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png 2x" width="150"/></a></div>
<div style="text-align:left; background-color:transparent; line-height:110%;">
<div id="annotation_120x5" style="position:absolute; left:120px; top:5px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px blue solid; padding:1px .6em; background-color:blue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> = Septa</span></span></div>
<div id="annotation_120x35" style="position:absolute; left:120px; top:35px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px silver solid; padding:1px .6em; background-color:yellow; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> = <a href="/wiki/Siphuncle" title="Siphuncle">Siphuncle</a></span></span></div>
</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="598" data-file-width="607" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png/150px-Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png/225px-Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png/300px-Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png 2x" width="150"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nautiloid_septa_n_siphuncle_01.png" title="File:Nautiloid septa n siphuncle 01.png"></a></div>
Septa and <a href="/wiki/Siphuncle" title="Siphuncle">siphuncle</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nautiloid" title="Nautiloid">nautiloid</a> shell</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volborthella" title="Volborthella">Volborthella</a></i>, some fossils of which predate <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=530">530</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, was long thought to be a cephalopod, but discoveries of more detailed fossils showed its shell was not secreted, but built from grains of the mineral <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> (silica), and it was not divided into a series of compartments by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Septum_(marine_biology)" title="Septum (marine biology)">septa</a> as those of fossil shelled cephalopods and the living <i><a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">Nautilus</a></i> are. <i>Volborthella</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s classification is uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> The Late Cambrian fossil <i><a href="/wiki/Plectronoceras" title="Plectronoceras">Plectronoceras</a></i> is now thought to be the earliest clearly cephalopod fossil, as its shell had septa and a <a href="/wiki/Siphuncle" title="Siphuncle">siphuncle</a>, a strand of tissue that <i>Nautilus</i> uses to remove water from compartments it has vacated as it grows, and which is also visible in fossil <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonite" title="Ammonite">ammonite</a> shells. However, <i>Plectronoceras</i> and other early cephalopods crept along the seafloor instead of swimming, as their shells contained a "ballast" of stony deposits on what is thought to be the underside, and had stripes and blotches on what is thought to be the upper surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> All cephalopods with external shells except the <a href="/wiki/Nautiloid" title="Nautiloid">nautiloids</a> became extinct by the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=65">65</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarshallWard1996_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarshallWard1996-67">[66]</a></sup> However, the shell-less <a href="/wiki/Coleoidea" title="Coleoidea">Coleoidea</a> (<a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a>) are abundant today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Early Cambrian fossils <i><a href="/wiki/Fordilla" title="Fordilla">Fordilla</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pojetaia" title="Pojetaia">Pojetaia</a></i> are regarded as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> "Modern-looking" bivalves appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a> period, <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=488–443">488 to 443</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> One bivalve group, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rudist" title="Rudist">rudists</a>, became major <a href="/wiki/Reef" title="Reef">reef</a>-builders in the Cretaceous, but became extinct in the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> Even so, bivalves remain abundant and diverse.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hyolitha" title="Hyolitha">Hyolitha</a> are a class of extinct animals with a shell and <a href="/wiki/Operculum_(gastropod)" title="Operculum (gastropod)">operculum</a> that may be molluscs. Authors who suggest they deserve their own <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phylum</a> do not comment on the position of this phylum in the tree of life<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sumrall2009_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sumrall2009-75">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Phylogeny">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="thumbcaption">A possible "family tree" of molluscs (2007).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SigwartSutton2007DeepMolluscanPhylogeny_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-SigwartSutton2007DeepMolluscanPhylogeny-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> Does not include <a href="/wiki/Annelid" title="Annelid">annelid</a> worms as the analysis concentrated on fossilizable "hard" features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SigwartSutton2007DeepMolluscanPhylogeny_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-SigwartSutton2007DeepMolluscanPhylogeny-76">[75]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> (evolutionary "family tree") of molluscs is a controversial subject. In addition to the debates about whether <i><a href="/wiki/Kimberella" title="Kimberella">Kimberella</a></i> and any of the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halwaxiid" title="Halwaxiid">halwaxiids</a>" were molluscs or closely related to molluscs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FedonkinEtAl2007NewDataOnKimberella_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FedonkinEtAl2007NewDataOnKimberella-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butterfield2006_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Butterfield2006-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CaronScheltemaSchanderRudkin2006_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-CaronScheltemaSchanderRudkin2006-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butterfield2008EarlyCambrianRadula_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Butterfield2008EarlyCambrianRadula-57">[56]</a></sup> debates arise about the relationships between the classes of living molluscs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sigwart2007_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sigwart2007-55">[54]</a></sup> In fact, some groups traditionally classified as molluscs may have to be redefined as distinct but related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoloboffEtAl2009_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoloboffEtAl2009-78">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Molluscs are generally regarded members of the <a href="/wiki/Lophotrochozoa" title="Lophotrochozoa">Lophotrochozoa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SigwartSutton2007DeepMolluscanPhylogeny_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-SigwartSutton2007DeepMolluscanPhylogeny-76">[75]</a></sup> a group defined by having <a href="/wiki/Trochophore" title="Trochophore">trochophore</a> larvae and, in the case of living <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lophophorata" title="Lophophorata">Lophophorata</a>, a feeding structure called a <a href="/wiki/Lophophore" title="Lophophore">lophophore</a>. The other members of the Lophotrochozoa are the <a href="/wiki/Annelid" title="Annelid">annelid</a> worms and seven marine <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> The diagram on the right summarizes a phylogeny presented in 2007.</p>
<p>Because the relationships between the members of the family tree are uncertain, it is difficult to identify the features inherited from the last common ancestor of all molluscs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry2004_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henry2004-80">[79]</a></sup> For example, it is uncertain whether the ancestral mollusc was <a href="/wiki/Metamerism_(biology)" title="Metamerism (biology)">metameric</a> (composed of repeating units)—if it was, that would suggest an origin from an <a href="/wiki/Annelid" title="Annelid">annelid</a>-like worm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs2000_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs2000-81">[80]</a></sup> Scientists disagree about this: Giribet and colleagues concluded, in 2006, the repetition of gills and of the foot's retractor muscles were later developments,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GiribetOkusuEtAlMolluscsWithSeriallyRepeatedStructures_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-GiribetOkusuEtAlMolluscsWithSeriallyRepeatedStructures-7">[6]</a></sup> while in 2007, Sigwart concluded the ancestral mollusc was metameric, and it had a foot used for creeping and a "shell" that was mineralized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sigwart2007_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sigwart2007-55">[54]</a></sup> In one particular branch of the family tree, the shell of <a href="/wiki/Conchifera" title="Conchifera">conchiferans</a> is thought to have evolved from the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/spicule" title="wikt:spicule">spicules</a> (small spines) of <a href="/wiki/Aplacophora" title="Aplacophora">aplacophorans</a>; but this is difficult to reconcile with the <a href="/wiki/Embryology" title="Embryology">embryological</a> origins of spicules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry2004_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Henry2004-80">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The molluscan shell appears to have originated from a mucus coating, which eventually stiffened into a <a href="/wiki/Cuticle" title="Cuticle">cuticle</a>. This would have been impermeable and thus forced the development of more sophisticated respiratory apparatus in the form of gills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RunnegarPojeta1974_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-RunnegarPojeta1974-48">[47]</a></sup> Eventually, the cuticle would have become mineralized,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RunnegarPojeta1974_48-4"><a href="#cite_note-RunnegarPojeta1974-48">[47]</a></sup> using the same genetic machinery (<a href="/wiki/Engrailed_(gene)" title="Engrailed (gene)">engrailed</a>) as most other bilaterian <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeletons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs2000_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs2000-81">[80]</a></sup> The first mollusc shell almost certainly was reinforced with the mineral <a href="/wiki/Aragonite" title="Aragonite">aragonite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The evolutionary relationships 'within' the molluscs are also debated, and the diagrams below show two widely supported reconstructions:</p>

<div class="thumbcaption">The "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Testaria" title="Testaria">Testaria</a>" hypothesis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SigwartSutton2007DeepMolluscanPhylogeny_76-4"><a href="#cite_note-SigwartSutton2007DeepMolluscanPhylogeny-76">[75]</a></sup></div>
