<p>Morphological analyses tend to recover a conchiferan clade that receives less support from molecular analyses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winnepenninckx_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winnepenninckx-83">[82]</a></sup> although these results also lead to unexpected paraphylies, for instance scattering the bivalves throughout all other mollusc groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Passamaneck2004_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Passamaneck2004-84">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, an analysis in 2009 using both <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular phylogenetics</a> comparisons concluded the molluscs are not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a>; in particular, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scaphopoda" title="Scaphopoda">Scaphopoda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bivalvia" title="Bivalvia">Bivalvia</a> are both separate, monophyletic lineages unrelated to the remaining molluscan classes; the traditional phylum Mollusca is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyphyletic" title="Polyphyletic">polyphyletic</a>, and it can only be made monophyletic if scaphopods and bivalves are excluded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoloboffEtAl2009_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-GoloboffEtAl2009-78">[77]</a></sup> A 2010 analysis recovered the traditional conchiferan and aculiferan groups, and showed molluscs were monophyletic, demonstrating that available data for solenogastres was contaminated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Current molecular data are insufficient to constrain the molluscan phylogeny, and since the methods used to determine the confidence in clades are prone to overestimation, it is risky to place too much emphasis even on the areas of which different studies agree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagele2009_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wagele2009-86">[85]</a></sup> Rather than eliminating unlikely relationships, the latest studies add new permutations of internal molluscan relationships, even bringing the conchiferan hypothesis into question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vinther2011_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vinther2011-87">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_interaction">Human interaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Human interaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Molluscs_in_culture" title="Molluscs in culture">Molluscs in culture</a></div>
<p>For millennia, molluscs have been a source of food for humans, as well as important luxury goods, notably <a href="/wiki/Pearl" title="Pearl">pearls</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mother_of_pearl" title="Mother of pearl">mother of pearl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyrian_purple" title="Tyrian purple">Tyrian purple</a> dye, <a href="/wiki/Sea_silk" title="Sea silk">sea silk</a>, and chemical compounds. Their shells have also been used as a form of <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currency</a> in some preindustrial societies. A number of species of molluscs can bite or sting humans, and some have become agricultural pests.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_by_humans">Uses by humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Uses by humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Seashell" title="Seashell">Seashell</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_edible_molluscs" title="List of edible molluscs">List of edible molluscs</a></div>
<p>Molluscs, especially bivalves such as <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">clams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a>, have been an important food source since at least the advent of anatomically modern humans, and this has often resulted in overfishing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> Other commonly eaten molluscs include <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopuses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whelk" title="Whelk">whelks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oysters" title="Oysters">oysters</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scallops" title="Scallops">scallops</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> In 2005, China accounted for 80% of the global mollusc catch, netting almost 11,000,000 tonnes (11,000,000 long tons; 12,000,000 short tons). Within Europe, France remained the industry leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> Some countries regulate importation and handling of molluscs and other <a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a>, mainly to minimize the poison risk from <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a> that can sometimes accumulate in the animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pearl_farm_(Seram,_Indonesia).jpg"><img alt="Photo of three circular metal cages in shallows, with docks, boathouses and palm trees in background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Pearl_farm_%28Seram%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg/220px-Pearl_farm_%28Seram%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Pearl_farm_%28Seram%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg/330px-Pearl_farm_%28Seram%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Pearl_farm_%28Seram%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg/440px-Pearl_farm_%28Seram%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pearl_farm_(Seram,_Indonesia).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saltwater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pearl_oyster" title="Pearl oyster">pearl oyster</a> farm in Seram, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most molluscs with shells can produce pearls, but only the pearls of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a> and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a>, whose shells are lined with <a href="/wiki/Nacre" title="Nacre">nacre</a>, are valuable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGastropoda_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaGastropoda-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaBivalvia_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaBivalvia-16">[15]</a></sup> The best natural pearls are produced by marine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pearl_oyster" title="Pearl oyster">pearl oysters</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pinctada" title="Pinctada">Pinctada</a> margaritifera</i> and <i>Pinctada mertensi</i>, which live in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical" title="Tropical">tropical</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical" title="Subtropical">subtropical</a> waters of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>. Natural pearls form when a small foreign object gets stuck between the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(mollusc)" title="Mantle (mollusc)">mantle</a> and shell.</p>
<p>The two methods of culturing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pearls" title="Pearls">pearls</a> insert either "seeds" or beads into oysters. The "seed" method uses grains of ground shell from freshwater <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a>, and overharvesting for this purpose has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endangered" title="Endangered">endangered</a> several freshwater mussel species in the southeastern United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaBivalvia_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004MolluscaBivalvia-16">[15]</a></sup> The pearl industry is so important in some areas, significant sums of money are spent on monitoring the health of farmed molluscs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna.jpg"><img alt="Mosaic of mustachioed, curly-haired man wearing crown and surrounded by halo" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1907" data-file-width="1409" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna.jpg/170px-Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna.jpg/255px-Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna.jpg/340px-Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> clad in <a href="/wiki/Tyrian_purple" title="Tyrian purple">Tyrian purple</a> and wearing numerous pearls</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Other luxury and high-<a href="/wiki/Social_status" title="Social status">status</a> products were made from molluscs. <a href="/wiki/Tyrian_purple" title="Tyrian purple">Tyrian purple</a>, made from the ink glands of <a href="/wiki/Murex" title="Murex">murex</a> shells, "... fetched its weight in silver" in the fourth century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_Christ" title="Before Christ">BC</a>, according to <a href="/wiki/Theopompus" title="Theopompus">Theopompus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> The discovery of large numbers of <i>Murex</i> shells on <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> suggests the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minoans" title="Minoans">Minoans</a> may have pioneered the extraction of "imperial purple" during the Middle Minoan period in the 20th–18th centuries BC, centuries before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrians" title="Tyrians">Tyrians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sea_silk" title="Sea silk">Sea silk</a> is a fine, rare, and valuable <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">fabric</a> produced from the long silky threads (<a href="/wiki/Byssus" title="Byssus">byssus</a>) secreted by several bivalve molluscs, particularly <i><a href="/wiki/Pinna_nobilis" title="Pinna nobilis">Pinna nobilis</a></i>, to attach themselves to the sea bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Procopius" title="Procopius">Procopius</a>, writing on the Persian wars <i>circa</i> 550 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, "stated that the five hereditary satraps (governors) of <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> who received their insignia from the Roman Emperor were given <a href="/wiki/Chlamys" title="Chlamys">chlamys</a> (or cloaks) made from <i>lana pinna</i>. Apparently, only the ruling classes were allowed to wear these chlamys."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mollusc shells, including those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cowrie" title="Cowrie">cowries</a>, were used as a kind of <a href="/wiki/Money" title="Money">money</a> (<a href="/wiki/Shell_money" title="Shell money">shell money</a>) in several preindustrial societies. However, these "currencies" generally differed in important ways from the standardized government-backed and -controlled money familiar to industrial societies. Some shell "currencies" were not used for commercial transactions, but mainly as <a href="/wiki/Social_status" title="Social status">social status</a> displays at important occasions, such as weddings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> When used for commercial transactions, they functioned as <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodity</a> money, as a tradable commodity whose value differed from place to place, often as a result of difficulties in transport, and which was vulnerable to incurable <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> if more efficient transport or "goldrush" behavior appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bioindicators">Bioindicators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Bioindicators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Bivalve molluscs are used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioindicators" title="Bioindicators">bioindicators</a> to monitor the health of aquatic environments in both fresh water and the marine environments. Their population status or structure, physiology, behaviour or the level of contamination with elements or compounds can indicate the state of contamination status of the ecosystem. They are particularly useful since they are sessile so that they are representative of the environment where they are sampled or placed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Molluscan_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Molluscan-100">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harmful_to_humans">Harmful to humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Harmful to humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stings_and_bites">Stings and bites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Stings and bites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hapalochlaena_lunulata.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1470" data-file-width="1183" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Hapalochlaena_lunulata.JPG/170px-Hapalochlaena_lunulata.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Hapalochlaena_lunulata.JPG/255px-Hapalochlaena_lunulata.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Hapalochlaena_lunulata.JPG/340px-Hapalochlaena_lunulata.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hapalochlaena_lunulata.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Blue-ringed_octopus" title="Blue-ringed octopus">blue-ringed octopus</a>'s rings are a warning signal; this octopus is alarmed, and its bite can kill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVRU_BlueRinged_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVRU_BlueRinged-101">[100]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some molluscs sting or bite, but deaths from mollusc venoms total less than 10% of those from <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a> stings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilliamsonFennerEtAl1996VenomousMarine_Animals_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-WilliamsonFennerEtAl1996VenomousMarine_Animals-102">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>All octopuses are venomous,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson1995GiantPacificOctopus_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson1995GiantPacificOctopus-103">[102]</a></sup> but only a few species pose a significant threat to humans. <a href="/wiki/Blue-ringed_octopus" title="Blue-ringed octopus">Blue-ringed octopuses</a> in the genus <i>Hapalochlaena</i>, which live around Australia and New Guinea, bite humans only if severely provoked,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVRU_BlueRinged_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVRU_BlueRinged-101">[100]</a></sup> but their venom kills 25% of human victims. Another tropical species, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octopus_apollyon" title="Octopus apollyon">Octopus apollyon</a></i>, causes severe <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> that can last for over a month even if treated correctly,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> and the bite of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Pacific_Red_Octopus" title="East Pacific Red Octopus">Octopus rubescens</a></i> can cause <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">necrosis</a> that lasts longer than one month if untreated, and headaches and weakness persisting for up to a week even if treated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_1999_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson_1999-105">[104]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Textile_cone.JPG"><img alt="Photo of cone on ocean bottom" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Textile_cone.JPG/220px-Textile_cone.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Textile_cone.JPG/330px-Textile_cone.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Textile_cone.JPG/440px-Textile_cone.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Textile_cone.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Live <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cone_shell" title="Cone shell">cone snails</a> can be dangerous to shell collectors, but are useful to <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurology</a> researchers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Scientist19October1996DoctorSnail_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_Scientist19October1996DoctorSnail-106">[105]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All species of <a href="/wiki/Cone_snail" title="Cone snail">cone snails</a> are venomous and can sting painfully when handled, although many species are too small to pose much of a risk to humans, and only a few fatalities have been reliably reported. Their venom is a complex mixture of <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a>, some fast-acting and others slower but deadlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Scientist19October1996DoctorSnail_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-New_Scientist19October1996DoctorSnail-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilliamsonFennerEtAl1996VenomousMarine_Animals_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-WilliamsonFennerEtAl1996VenomousMarine_Animals-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> The effects of individual cone-shell toxins on victims' nervous systems are so precise as to be useful tools for research in <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurology</a>, and the small size of their <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> makes it easy to synthesize them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Scientist19October1996DoctorSnail_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-New_Scientist19October1996DoctorSnail-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Disease_vectors">Disease vectors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Disease vectors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schistosomiasis_itch.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1204" data-file-width="1795" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Schistosomiasis_itch.jpeg/220px-Schistosomiasis_itch.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Schistosomiasis_itch.jpeg/330px-Schistosomiasis_itch.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Schistosomiasis_itch.jpeg/440px-Schistosomiasis_itch.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schistosomiasis_itch.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesicle_(dermatology)" title="Vesicle (dermatology)">Skin vesicles</a> created by the penetration of <i><a href="/wiki/Schistosoma" title="Schistosoma">Schistosoma</a></i>. (Source: <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">CDC</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Schistosomiasis" title="Schistosomiasis">Schistosomiasis</a> (also known as bilharzia, bilharziosis or snail fever), a disease caused by the fluke worm <i><a href="/wiki/Schistosoma" title="Schistosoma">Schistosoma</a></i>, is "second only to malaria as the most devastating parasitic disease in tropical countries. An estimated 200 million people in 74 countries are infected with the disease – 100 million in Africa alone."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> The parasite has 13 known species, two of which infect humans. The parasite itself is not a mollusc, but all the species have freshwater snails as <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">intermediate hosts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests">Pests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mollusca&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Pests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some species of molluscs, particularly certain snails and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slugs" title="Slugs">slugs</a>, can be serious crop pests,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> and when introduced into new environments, can unbalance local <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>. One such pest, the giant African snail <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achatina_fulica" title="Achatina fulica">Achatina fulica</a></i>, has been introduced to many parts of Asia, as well as to many islands in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>. In the 1990s, this species reached the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a>. Attempts to control it by introducing the predatory snail <i><a href="/wiki/Euglandina_rosea" title="Euglandina rosea">Euglandina rosea</a></i> proved disastrous, as the predator ignored <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achatina_fulica" title="Achatina fulica">Achatina fulica</a></i> and went on to extirpate several native snail species, instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup></p>
